<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/1463-9084/1999">Phys. Chem. Chem.     </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      8th January 2022
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 21st March 2022</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 21st March 2022</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>As novel post lithium-ion batteries and promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) suffering from the limited Li resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are nowadays emerging and show bright prospects in large-scale energy storage applications due to abundant Na resources. However, a lack of suitable anode materials has become a key obstacle for the development of SIBs. Here we explore the potential of the two-dimensional (2D) YC space and identify a novel anode material for SIBs, a new Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet with <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 crystal symmetry, by means of first-principles swarm structure calculations. This Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure has rather good kinetic and thermodynamic stability, possesses intrinsic metallicity, and remains metallic after adsorbing Na atoms, ensuring good electrical conductivity during the SIB cycle. Remarkably, a Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet as an anode for SIBs possesses the essential properties of a high specific capacity (752 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), a low barrier energy (0.1 eV), and suitable open-circuit voltage (00.15 V). These characteristics are comparable and even superior to those of another known 2D Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C anode material, indicating that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can act as an appealing new candidate as an anode material for SIBs and offering new insights into the 2D YC space.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect274"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Due to the burning of fossil fuels, the situations of environmental pollution and the energy crisis have become increasingly severe. In past decades, there has been a continuous effort to exploit and develop renewable energy.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Among various reported sustainable energy storage systems, rechargeable metal-ion (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, Li, Na, K) batteries are expected to be widely used in portable electronic products and electric vehicles.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> At present, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as one of the most successful sustainable energy storage devices, are widely used in practice. However, the storage capacity of Li on the earth is relatively low. Due to the wide application of LIBs, the consumption of Li resources is huge, and the existing lithium resources may only last for several decades.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">68</span></sup></a> In contrast, Na is more abundant and more evenly distributed on earth than Li. Additionally, due to their similar chemical properties, SIBs share similar operational principles and battery devices to LIBs, and are considered post lithium-ion batteries. Meanwhile, the mature LIB technology can be migrated to sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1,9,10</span></sup></a> Regarding these, SIBs could be seen as an attractive alternative to LIBs, which can reduce reliance on depleted Li resources.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">Nowadays, SIBs are still in an initial development stage. A key obstacle restricting the development of SIBs is the lack of a suitable anode material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1214</span></sup></a> As we know, some reported anode materials in LIBs are not applicable to SIBs. For example, graphite is a common anode material for LIBs, but the diameter of Na atoms is larger than that of Li atoms, and the distance between graphite layers (0.34 nm) is too narrow to accommodate Na.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1517</span></sup></a> Thus, searching for suitable anode materials with desirable properties is becoming rather urgent for the development of SIBs today.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Considering the obvious advantages of 2D materials, such as open ion transportation pathways, large surface area, and enriched active sites,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1820</span></sup></a> they show enormous potential as anode materials for SIBs. 2D transition metal carbides (MXenes) are among the latest additions to the 2D materials world, which have recently proved to be excellent anode materials in LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21</span></sup></a> As a typical MXene, the reported Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C sheet with a similar structure to a Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>N sheet was found to hold both electronic states and good electrochemical performance as an anode material for SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> Significant interactions between the Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C sheet and an Na atom ensure its high theoretical specific capacity (564 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), high Na mobility, and small average open-circuit voltage. Considering these characteristics, the 2D YC system is expected to store other suitable materials as anode materials for SIBs, becoming our research focus here.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">To gain a computational handle on the above idea, herein, we employ a swarm-intelligence structure prediction method to search the thermodynamically stable 2D structural candidates of various possible YC compositions. As expected, we identified a new composition of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> with a thermodynamically stable <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 crystal structure in the 2D YC space. By means of first-principles computations, we further evaluated the potential of using the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet as an anode material for SIBs. The obtained results show that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet possesses the desired performances for an anode material for SIBs, exhibiting high conductivity, good adsorption ability, high storage capacity, and a fast charge/discharge rate for Na-ions. These intriguing characteristics are comparable to those of the known Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C sheet. This finding provides us with a new candidate for SIB anode materials in 2D YC space.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect305"><span class="a_heading">2. Computational details</span></h2>
      <span>The swarm-intelligence structure search method as implemented in the CALYPSO code<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> was adopted and employed here to search for potential new 2D YC structural candidates, to find stable or metastable structures depending on the given chemical composition, and its effectiveness has been verified by a large number of applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2331</span></sup></a> A vacuum layer of about 20  was set to avoid interaction between adjacent layers. The geometrical optimizations and electronic structure calculations within the generalized gradient approximation (GGA)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> were performed at the level of density functional theory (DFT) using the Vienna <span class="italic">Ab initio</span> Simulation Package (VASP).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27,28</span></sup></a> The electron projector augmented wave (PAW)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> potentials of Y and C treated 4d<small><sup>1</sup></small>5s<small><sup>2</sup></small> and 2s<small><sup>2</sup></small>2p<small><sup>2</sup></small> electrons as the valence electrons, respectively. A plane wave cut-off energy of 500 eV was used, and the Brillouin zone was sampled with a resolution of 2  0.025 <small><sup>1</sup></small> using the MonkhorstPack method.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> van der Waals interaction was taken into account using the semiempirical DFT-D2 approach.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> The transition states were located by computing the minimum-energy path (MEP) for the Na diffusion processes using the nudged elastic band (NEB) method,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35,36</span></sup></a> which starts by inserting a series of image structures between the initial and final states of the reaction.</span>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect323"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>We performed extensive 2D global minimum structural searches for typical YC compositions with various Y/C ratios. After identifying the 2D crystal phases and computing their energies to determine the most viable structure at each composition, a number of low energy phases were obtained in these YC compositions. To evaluate the relative thermodynamic stability of these different YC compositions, we calculated their formation energies and constructed a convex hull based on the obtained results (Fig. S1, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). By assessing the convex hull, a new composition of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> possessing good thermodynamic stability was uncovered in the 2D space. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> geometric structure has <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 crystal symmetry with the lowest energy. Viewing its architecture, the basic building blocks of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> can be considered a YC coordinated multilayer with a stacked order of YCYCYCY, in which C atoms are 6-fold coordinated with adjacent Y atoms. The optimized lattice parameters are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>.</span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 crystal structure of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet; the pink balls represent Y atoms and the green balls represent C atoms. (b) The ELF maps sliced in the (1 0 0) direction of the <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure. (c) Phonon dispersion curves of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure. (d) Decomposed PDOS of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure, projected on three orbitals (s, p and d) of Y and projected on two orbitals (s and p) of the C atom.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>

                <td align="char" char="." valign="top">0.6667</td>
                <td align="char" char="." valign="top">0.3333</td>
                <td align="char" char="." valign="top">0.3600</td>
              </tr>
              <tr align="left">
                <td valign="top"> = 120</td>
                <td valign="top">
                </td>
                <td valign="top">
                </td>
                <td valign="top">
                </td>
                <td valign="top">
                </td>
              </tr>
            </tbody>
          </table></div></div>
        <hr class="hrule"><br>
      <p class="otherpara">In order to reveal the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 bond-forming property, we carried out its electronic local function (ELF) calculation. As we know, an ELF value of &gt;0.5 generally corresponds to having more electrons or covalent bonds, while an ELF value of 0.5 generally corresponds to an ionic bond.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>, the <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 ELF maps show that there is a very strong ionic interaction between Y and C in the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> structure, in which electron transfer would occur from the Y atom to the C atom. Meanwhile, note that the bond length between Y and C is about 2.35 , which is less than the total distance of their covalent radii, indicating a weak YC covalent bond. This ionic/covalent YC bond characteristic of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> structure allows its <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 configuration to maintain good stability.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">We further investigated the dynamic stability of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure by calculating its phonon dispersion curve. The obtained phonon spectrum shows no imaginary frequency, as can be seen in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>, indicating that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure possesses good dynamic stability. It is worth noting that the highest frequency of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure is beyond 1600 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>, much higher than that of an MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sheet (470 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> indicating that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure has stronger chemical bonds. Moreover, by performing <span class="italic">ab initio</span> molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations in the canonical ensemble,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> the thermal stability of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure was evaluated at room temperature (300 K). As shown in Fig. S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), this sheet well maintains its structural integrity with no deformation, indicating good stability against thermal fluctuation at room temperature. These simulations demonstrate that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure has outstanding thermal stability and is feasible for practical applications.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Cohesive energy is a well-accepted parameter to evaluate the feasibility of the experimental synthesis of predicted 2D materials;<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> therefore, the cohesive energy of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure was further assessed and compared. The result shows it is 3.42 eV per atom, which is very comparable to that of the synthesized 2D GeP<small><sub>3</sub></small> (3.34 eV per atom)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> and black phosphorus (3.48 eV per atom).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> These results indicate that our proposed Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet has a high possibility of being synthesized under certain experimental conditions. In experiments, some MXenes can be produced by a kinetically controlled etching process at room temperature. Like other MXenes, here our proposed Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> composition could possibly be chemically etched from a general formula of M<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span>+1</sub></small>X<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>T<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small> (<span class="italic">n</span> = 13), where M represents an early transition metal, X is carbon and/or nitrogen and T<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small> stands for the surface terminations (for example, OH, O and/or F terminations).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> To check whether this is possible, a surface-functionalized Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure with an OH terminal group was considered and investigated as an example, in addition to the evaluation of the preparation of bare Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> We compared their energies and calculated the phonon dispersion of the functionalized Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure (Fig. S3, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). From the obtained results, the energetically most stable structure of bare Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> and dynamically stable terminations Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>(OH)<small><sub>2</sub></small> support the idea that Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> MXenes could be etched from its surface-functionalized structure.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Good conductivity is a necessary prerequisite for electrode materials to achieve excellent electrochemical performance. To illustrate the conductivity of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure, we calculated its band structure and projected density of states (PDOS). In the energy band diagram of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure shown in Fig. S4 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the energy level has crossed the Fermi level and exhibits a flat-band dispersion in the vicinity of the <span class="italic"></span> point along the <span class="italic"></span><span class="italic">M</span> direction at the Fermi level. Such a Mexican-hat-like feature of the band structure results in an intrinsically metallic state with high conductivity. As can be seen in the PDOS in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>, the metal states of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure near the Fermi level are very high and near the Fermi level with a high carrier density, which is contributed mainly by the 4d orbital of the Y atom and the 2p orbital of the C atom. This excellent electrical conductivity indicates that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can meet the prerequisites to be used as an electrode material.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">As stated above, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can be expected to become a new anode material for SIBs. To assess its potential for SIB application, we firstly studied the adsorption ability of a single metal Na atom on its surface. According to the definition of adsorption energy,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> the more negative the adsorption energy, the stronger the interaction between the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> structure and metal atoms, and the easier it is for metal atoms to be adsorbed. Considering the symmetry of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 membrane structure, several representative adsorption sites are selected as the starting geometry. For each site, we calculated its adsorption energy to determine the most favorable energy adsorption configuration. After full geometrical optimizations, we find only three inequivalent stable-adsorption sites, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>: two sites H<small><sub>1</sub></small> and H<small><sub>2</sub></small> on the top of two non-equivalent Y atoms, and H<small><sub>C</sub></small> on the hollow C atom. The adsorption energies for metal Na ions at these three sites are 0.02 eV, 0.13 eV, and 0.15 eV, respectively. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>, the results show that Na adsorbed on H<small><sub>C</sub></small> sites, having the lowest adsorption energy, is the most favorable energy adsorption configuration. Compared with the H<small><sub>C</sub></small> site, the adsorption energies of the H<small><sub>1</sub></small> and H<small><sub>2</sub></small> sites are higher, which may be caused by the fact that the H<small><sub>1</sub></small> and H<small><sub>2</sub></small> sites are close to the metal Y atom, so that the Na ions suffer repulsion from them. Moreover, the band structure diagram of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na shows that the material still exhibits metallic properties after the adsorption of Na ions and its conductivity even becomes stronger (Fig. S5, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). In short, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can spontaneously absorb Na atoms and retain strong conductivity, providing a very ideal property for application as SIB anodes.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Top view of the geometric structures for Na atom adsorption on the H<small><sub>1</sub></small>, H<small><sub>C</sub></small>, and H<small><sub>2</sub></small> sites of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet. The yellow ball represents the metal Na atom. (b) The adsorption energies of metal Na ions at the three sites. (c) Migration Paths (I, II, III) and (d) diffusion barrier profiles for Na on the surface of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet. The Path I, II and III labels represent H<small><sub>C</sub></small>  H<small><sub>2</sub></small>  H<small><sub>C</sub></small>, H<small><sub>C</sub></small>  H<small><sub>C</sub></small>, and H<small><sub>C</sub></small>  H<small><sub>1</sub></small>  H<small><sub>C</sub></small> paths, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The mobility of metal atoms on the electrode material is an important index to measure its rate performance and determine the chargedischarge rate capacities of rechargeable batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46,47</span></sup></a> Thus, we further calculated the mobility of the metal Na ion on the surface of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure to determine the diffusion path with the minimum energy. The most stable adsorption configurations on two adjacent H<small><sub>C</sub></small> sites were set as the initial state and the final state, respectively, which can be determined as a possible migration path. By spreading through an H<small><sub>1</sub></small> site or H<small><sub>2</sub></small> site between two adjacent H<small><sub>C</sub></small> sites, the other possible migration paths can be determined. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a>, it is obvious that diffusion between two adjacent H<small><sub>C</sub></small> sites has three possible paths: that is H<small><sub>C</sub></small>  H<small><sub>2</sub></small>  H<small><sub>C</sub></small> (Path I), H<small><sub>C</sub></small>  H<small><sub>C</sub></small> (Path II), and H<small><sub>C</sub></small>  H<small><sub>1</sub></small>  H<small><sub>C</sub></small> (Path III). Based on the NEB method, we estimated the corresponding diffusion barriers of these three pathways for one Na ion on the surface of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>). Among them, the migration barrier of Path I is the lowest with 0.10 eV (the migration barriers of Path II and Path III are 0.14 eV and 0.20 eV, respectively), which means Na-ions are most likely to move along this path. In comparison with other reported MXenes, this Na-ion diffusion barrier of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet is comparable to some typical MXenes such as Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C, Ti<small><sub>2</sub></small>C, and Ti<small><sub>3</sub></small>C<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a>). This low diffusion energy barrier reflects the fast mobility of Na-ions on the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet as an anode materials for SIBs.</p>

        <hr class="hrule"><br>
      <p class="otherpara">After determining the optimal adsorption site and diffusion barrier of Na, we focused on an investigation of the Na maximum theoretical storage capacity in the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet, which is a key index to measure the power density of metal-ion batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48,49</span></sup></a> In order to estimate the maximum possible storage of Na, we investigated the concentration-dependent adsorption behavior of Na atoms on both sides of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet. Specifically, various concentrations of multilayer adsorption (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>, Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>, <span class="italic">n</span> = 1, 2, 3) were considered (Fig. S6, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). To evaluate whether these different concentrations of Na atoms can be favorably adsorbed on the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet, we calculated both the average adsorption energy <a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-t2.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t2.gif"></a> and the differential adsorption energy (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sup>diff</sup></small><small><sub>H</sub></small>) of Na atoms on the Sc<small><sub>3</sub></small>Si<small><sub>2</sub></small> sheet (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>). The average adsorption energy for Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> is defined as:<br></p><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgugt3"><a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-t3.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td><a id="ugt3"><span id="ugt3"></span></a></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>where <span class="italic">E</span>(Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>) is the total energy of the most stable structure of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>, and <span class="italic">E</span>(Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>) is the total energy of a sheet structure that has adsorbed <span class="italic">n</span> Na atoms. If <a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-t4.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t4.gif"></a> is negative, this value indicates the thermodynamic preference for forming a specific Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> structure without Na atom clustering. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, the calculated <a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-t5.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t5.gif"></a> values for Na adsorption are negative at a very high concentration up to 11, showing no clustering problem. The differential adsorption energy for Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> can be obtained with the following equation:<br><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sup>diff</sup></small><small><sub>H</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span>(Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span>(Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span>1</sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span>(Na)</td></tr></tbody></table><span class="italic">E</span><small><sup>diff</sup></small><small><sub>H</sub></small> describes the energy released/needed when adding one more Na atom on the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span>1</sub></small> structure. According to this definition, a negative <span class="italic">E</span><small><sup>diff</sup></small><small><sub>H</sub></small> value suggests the favorable formation of the next atom adsorption. However, a small positive value does not exclude the formation of the next atom adsorption since this step can be considered an activation barrier to be overcome when forming the structure with one more adsorbed atom. The calculated <span class="italic">E</span><small><sup>diff</sup></small><small><sub>H</sub></small> values, except for the case of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>3</sub></small> with only a few slightly positive values (&lt;0.023 eV), almost remain negative. These values indicate the thermodynamic preference for forming a specific Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> structure and the favorable formation of a high Na concentration adsorption. To check the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> configurations, we found that the Na atoms can be absorbed on either side of the sheet, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>. The number of Na atoms absorbed in one Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> formula is just equal to the number of Na-absorbed layers. Such a phenomenon of Na multilayer absorptions seem likely as it has been reported in the Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C sheet.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a><p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The average adsorption energy <a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-t1.gif"></a> and the differential adsorption energy (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sup>diff</sup></small><small><sub>H</sub></small>) of sodiated-Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> configurations as a function of Na adsorption concentration. (b) Side view of the sodiated-Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> configurations (taking Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>3</sub></small> as an example) with just three layers (3L) of Na atoms on either side. (c) Hull of formation energies for the most stable configurations of an Na-adsorbed Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> monolayer. (d) The open-circuit voltage profile of the sodiated-Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> configurations as a function of Na adsorption concentration.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To identify the maximum adsorption stoichiometry of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>, we further examined the relative stability of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> through the calculated formation energies. A given adsorption conformer whose formation energy sits on the solid line of a convex hull is stable with respect to the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet, body-centered cubic Na solid, and the other adsorption conformers. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>, almost all these configurations of the Na adduct (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>, Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>, <span class="italic">n</span> = 111) are located on the solid line, so we can conclude that the maximum stable Na adsorption conformer can reach up to Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>11</sub></small>. Taking Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>11</sub></small> as an example, we calculated its theoretical storage capacity from <span class="italic">C</span><small><sub>M</sub></small> = (<span class="italic">n</span>  <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>max</sub></small>  <span class="italic">F</span>)/<span class="italic">M</span><small><sub>Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>11</sub></small></sub></small>, where <span class="italic">n</span> refers to the valence state of metal adatoms, <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>max</sub></small> is the maximum number of metal adatoms, <span class="italic">F</span> is the Faraday constant with a value of 26<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">801 mA h mol<small><sup>1</sup></small>, and <span class="italic">M</span><small><sub>Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>11</sub></small></sub></small> is the molar weight of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>11</sub></small>. The result shows that the maximum Na theoretical storage capacity can reach 752 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. For the maximum specific capacity, we compare Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> with other typical MXenes as anode materials for SIBs in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a>. The specific capacity of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> is higher than that of Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C (564 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and the experimental synthetic MXenes Ti<small><sub>3</sub></small>C<small><sub>2</sub></small> (370 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and Ti<small><sub>2</sub></small>C (359 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>).</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The open-circuit voltage (OCV) is also an important parameter in anode materials, reflecting the driving force to achieve battery equilibrium when connecting the cathode and anode. In addition, an OCV value in the range of 01.0 V prevents dendrite formation of alkali metals during the discharge/charge process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit50"><sup><span class="sup_ref">50</span></sup></a> The average OCV between the sodiation and desodiation processes in the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet are expressed by OCV = (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small></sub></small> + <span class="italic">nE</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small>  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small></sub></small>)/<span class="italic">nme</span>, where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small></sub></small>, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small>, and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small></sub></small> are the total energies of one unit cell of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet, an Na metal atom in a body-centered cubic structure, and the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small> sheet. And <span class="italic">m</span> represents the valence states of fully ionized Na-ions (<span class="italic">m</span> = 1 for Na<small><sup>1+</sup></small>) and <span class="italic">e</span> is the electronic charge of an Na-ion. We selected adsorbed Na-ion concentrations increasing from <span class="italic">n</span> = 0 to <span class="italic">n</span> = 11 and calculated the OCV values (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>). The results show that the OCV values gradually decrease from 0.15 eV to 0.019 eV in Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>, which all belong to an ideal potential range of 01.0 V for an anode material. This low OCV relieves the risk of dendrite formation and reveals that the change in Na intercalation voltage is steady.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">From the electronic point of view, we compared the ELF maps sliced in the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 (1 0 0) direction with two representative Na adsorption concentrations: <span class="italic">i.e.</span>, Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na with one layer of Na atoms (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a>) and Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>Na<small><sub>3</sub></small> with three layers of Na atoms (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>). It can be seen that more electrons are transferred from the inner layer to the outer layer, forming a negative electron cloud, which can effectively decrease the repulsion interactions among the adsorbed Na atoms and favor multilayer Na absorption. Such multilayer absorption has also been observed in other systems (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, Nb<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>2</sub></small>C,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a> B<small><sub>7</sub></small>P<small><sub>2</sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit57"><sup><span class="sup_ref">57</span></sup></a>), and makes an important contribution to the high capacity. Additionally, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet is thicker than the Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C sheet, so structural distortion is not easy after the adsorption of layered Na atoms. This benefit favors more multi-layer Na adsorption.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2cp00111j-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2022/CP/d2cp00111j/d2cp00111j-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">ELF maps sliced in the (1 0 0) direction of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet with one layer (1L) of Na atoms (a) and three layers (3L) of Na atoms (b). (c) Schematics of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet as an anode material of SIBs.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The above results for key performance parameters, including electrical and ionic conductivity, diffusion energy barrier, specific capacity, and OCV, all point to the fact that our proposed Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can be expected to exhibit the desired performance as an anode material of SIBs (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>). In addition, we also examined the potential of this Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet in other alkali metal-ion batteries (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, Li, K). It was found that this Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can also adsorb K atoms and the metal properties remain after adsorption (Fig. S7 and S8, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) and it has a low migration barrier (Fig. S9, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Unlike the Na-ion, the K-ion has a larger ionic radius and therefore the distance between adjacent K-ions becomes shorter with the same number of adsorbed ions. In that case, the repulsions between adjacent K-ions or Y would increase, which is attributed to a weak adsorption interaction between the K-ion and Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small>. In our simulation of K-ion adsorption, it was found that only one of two adjacent H<small><sub>C</sub></small> sites can adsorb a K-ion, while the other site cannot adsorb a K-ion due to K-ion repulsion (Fig. S8, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). As a result, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet adsorbs fewer K-ions (Fig. S10, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) with relatively low capacity. In comparison, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet is more suitable as an anode material for SIBs.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect944"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>In this work, we performed an extensive 2D YC exploration to explore its thermodynamically stable 2D structural candidates and possible application as an anode material for alkali metal-ion batteries. As expected, we identified a new composition of Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> with <span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 crystal symmetry. By means of first-principles computations, we systematically assessed the potential of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure as an anode material for SIBs. The obtained results show that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small><span class="italic">P</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>1 structure has rather good kinetic and thermodynamic stability and possesses intrinsic metallicity and remains metallic after adsorbing Na atoms. Importantly, it has excellent electrochemical properties, including a high specific capacity (752 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), a low barrier energy (0.1 eV), and a suitable open-circuit voltage (00.15 eV). These characteristics are comparable to those of another known anode material Y<small><sub>2</sub></small>C sheets for SIBs. In addition, we extended our work to examine the potential of the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet for other alkali metal ion batteries (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, Li, K). These results reveal that the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet can also adsorb K atoms, but it has low K adsorption capacity. Compared with the Li/K-ion patterns, the Y<small><sub>4</sub></small>C<small><sub>3</sub></small> sheet is more suitable as an anode material for SIBs. This finding will give new insights into the further search for anode materials for SIBs in the 2D YC space.</span>
